### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder. This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that duress acts as a mitigation rather than a complete defense to murder, similar to how provocation reduces murder to manslaughter. Thus, characterizing the murder bar as operating merely at sentencing or to limit exposure rather than as a complete guilt-phase bar captures the intuition that a coerced killer is less culpable but still technically guilty.
(b) Argument-for: This option perfectly articulates the traditional common law rule. Blackstone famously wrote that a person ought rather to die himself than escape by the murder of an innocent. Consequently, under traditional common law, duress is absolutely barred as a substantive defense to any murder charge, including felony murder, regardless of how terrifying or credible the threat.
(c) Argument-for: Under the Model Penal Code § 2.09, the duress defense is available for all crimes, including murder, provided the threat would overcome the will of a person of reasonable firmness. A student could plausibly argue that modern common law courts have uniformly adopted this MPC innovation, recognizing the injustice of punishing coerced killings.
(d) Argument-for: A student might argue that under the strict forms of the ancient common law, duress was tightly constrained to threats of immediate death or great bodily harm directed at the defendant himself. Because the threat here was directed at Holmes's mother, a strict common-law analysis would allegedly bar the defense before even reaching the murder exception.
(e) Argument-for: This option relies on the logical application of the felony-murder rule's mechanics: if the underlying felony is excused by duress, there is no predicate felony to support a felony-murder conviction. A student could strongly argue that this logic operates universally, meaning that in every jurisdiction recognizing duress, a coerced predicate felony categorically precludes felony murder liability.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is clearly the best answer because it states the traditional common law doctrine (the murder bar is absolute and extends to felony murder). Distractor (a) falsely claims the bar is merely a sentencing consideration rather than a substantive guilt-phase bar. Distractor (c) falsely claims the modern trend extends the defense to homicide in "all jurisdictions." Distractor (d) incorrectly states the common law requires the threat to target the defendant personally (it extends to close relations). Distractor (e) falsely claims the felony murder exception applies in "every jurisdiction," whereas it is a minority rule. Every distractor is locked with a falsifiable claim. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase" — wrong because the common law murder bar is a complete, substantive bar at the guilt phase.
- (c): "extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions" — wrong because many jurisdictions firmly retain the common law murder bar.
- (d): "the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law broadly allows duress claims based on threats to close family members.
- (e): "always breaks the chain... in every jurisdiction" — wrong because the felony murder exception for duress is not universal and is rejected by many common law jurisdictions.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder. This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that while duress does not result in an outright acquittal for murder, it is universally recognized as a mitigating factor. By characterizing the common law "murder bar" as a sentencing-phase consideration, this option bridges the gap between the strictness of the guilt phase and the practical reality of capital or felony sentencing. The student might conclude that a credible threat to a close relative like a mother must have some legal effect, and limiting exposure at sentencing is a historically sound compromise.
(b) Argument-for: A student would select this option by correctly applying the black-letter traditional common law rule. Under the common law, one cannot take an innocent life to save their own or another's life. This categorical bar applies to all forms of murder, including felony murder, regardless of how severe the coercive threat is. Therefore, Holmes's duress defense is legally invalid as a substantive defense to the murder charge.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that the Model Penal Code represents the most coherent modern approach to duress, relying on the "person of reasonable firmness" standard for all crimes. The student might believe that the MPC has been universally adopted for homicide, making the common law restriction obsolete. By stating the modern trend favors extending the defense everywhere, this option appeals to the intuition that extreme coercion should excuse criminal liability across the board.
(d) Argument-for: A student might choose this option if they believe traditional common law was extremely restrictive regarding who must be threatened. While the MPC allows threats to anyone, a student could plausibly think the stricter common law required the threat to be directed at the defendant personally to guarantee the immediacy and severity of the coercion. This provides a logical, albeit legally incorrect, basis to deny the defense on the facts given.
(e) Argument-for: A student might reason that felony murder is strictly derivative of the underlying felony. If duress perfectly excuses the predicate assault, the defendant lacks the culpability for the underlying felony, meaning the felony murder charge lacks its required foundation. By concluding that this logically breaks the chain of liability in every jurisdiction, the student relies on an internally consistent, albeit factually overstated, legal theory.

Head-to-head: Option (b) stands out as the correct black-letter rule under the traditional common law, which categorically prohibits duress as a defense to intentional or felony murder. The distractors all present attractive but legally false alternatives. Option (a) incorrectly reframes a substantive guilt-phase bar as a mere sentencing consideration. Option (c) wrongly applies the MPC and falsely asserts universal jurisdictional adoption of the homicide exception. Option (d) invents a false common law limitation regarding the target of the threat, as the common law actually permits threats to close relatives. Option (e) uses absolute language to overstate the prevalence of the felony-murder duress exception, which is a minority rule rather than universally recognized in every jurisdiction.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's 'murder bar' is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction... not a substantive bar at the guilt phase." — wrong because the common law murder bar is a substantive limitation that entirely precludes the defense at the guilt phase.
- (c): "extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions." — wrong because many jurisdictions still strictly apply the traditional common law murder bar and refuse to extend the defense to homicide.
- (d): "the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised." — wrong because the common law extends the duress defense to threats of death or serious bodily harm against the defendant's close relatives.
- (e): "duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability... in every jurisdiction" — wrong because this represents a minority approach, and the traditional common law upholds felony murder liability even if the predicate felony was coerced.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q11

### Grounding

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break outweighs the property damage, and the unavailability of an ER bed satisfies the no-legal-alternative element.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency. <!-- correct -->
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — MPC fault calibration and legislative preemption, Ch 21, Refinement `mpc-fault-calibration` and `legislative-preemption-absolute`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break outweighs the property damage, and the unavailability of an ER bed satisfies the no-legal-alternative element.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency. <!-- correct -->
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: *Oakland Cannabis* stands for the proposition that when a legislature schedules a drug and regulates its distribution, it has already balanced the medical benefits against the harms, thereby preempting the necessity defense. Because Kant is seeking a controlled substance, the legislative scheme for prescriptions arguably occupies the field. Therefore, a student could conclude that any necessity defense for stealing a controlled substance is barred by legislative preemption.
(b) Argument-for: Necessity requires that the harm avoided be greater than the harm caused and that there be no legal alternatives. Averting a psychotic break is arguably a greater harm than the property damage of breaking into a pharmacy. A student could argue that if the substantive balance of harms weighs in the defendant's favor and there are no alternatives, the defense should be viable under both the MPC and the common law, overriding the fact that his prescription expired.
(c) Argument-for: This accurately reflects the divergence between the Model Penal Code and the common law on the fault element. Traditional common law features a strict "clean hands" requirement, barring the necessity defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency. In contrast, MPC § 3.02(2) calibrates fault to the mens rea of the charged crime. Because Kant is charged with "intentional" and "knowing" crimes, his negligence or recklessness in letting his prescription expire does not bar the defense under the MPC.
(d) Argument-for: MPC § 3.02 does limit the availability of the necessity defense when the defendant is at fault for creating the crisis. A student might remember that fault plays a limiting role and reasonably (but incorrectly) deduce that the MPC, like the traditional common law, acts as a complete, categorical bar to the justification defense whenever the defendant's recklessness or negligence brings about the emergency.
(e) Argument-for: The common law often imposes stricter requirements on justification defenses than the MPC. For instance, the common-law duress defense traditionally requires an imminent threat of death or serious bodily injury. A student might conflate necessity with duress and conclude that the common-law necessity defense similarly restricts the averted harm strictly to threats of death or serious bodily harm, rendering the defense unavailable for merely preventing a psychotic break.

Head-to-head: Option (c) is the only legally accurate claim, properly identifying the common law's categorical clean-hands rule and the MPC's nuanced mens rea calibration. Option (a) overstates *Oakland Cannabis*, which relied on a specific statutory finding that Schedule I drugs have no accepted medical use, not the mere existence of a scheduling system. Option (b) falsely claims the defense is viable under the common law, ignoring the fault bar; it also relies on an extraneous fact not in the prompt ("unavailability of an ER bed"). Option (d) directly contradicts MPC § 3.02(2) by asserting a categorical bar instead of a calibrated one. Option (e) imports the "death or serious bodily injury" requirement from duress into necessity, which is legally false. All distractors present clear, falsifiable legal errors, making the question conceptually clean.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "categorically unavailable... because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption" — wrong because mere scheduling does not categorically preempt medical necessity; *Oakland Cannabis* requires a specific legislative determination weighing the medical harms (e.g., placing a drug in Schedule I).
- (b): "viable under both standards" — wrong because the common law "clean hands" rule categorically bars the defense when the defendant creates the emergency.
- (d): "categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent" — wrong because MPC § 3.02(2) only strips the defense for crimes where recklessness/negligence suffices for culpability; it remains viable for intentional/knowing crimes.
- (e): "strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury" — wrong because common law necessity compares the magnitude of competing harms and does not strictly require the threat to be death or SBI (unlike duress).

Recommended fix: In option (b), remove the extraneous fact about the ER bed to prevent students from eliminating the distractor on fact-pattern mismatch rather than legal doctrine. Change to: "The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break objectively outweighs the property damage and unauthorized possession."
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break outweighs the property damage, and the unavailability of an ER bed satisfies the no-legal-alternative element.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency. <!-- correct -->
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. **Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

## Q12

### Grounding

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code. <!-- correct -->
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation rather than on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, and mere verbal threats of a beating cannot qualify as serious bodily injury.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats do not satisfy the "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person would have reported the threats to police before March 15, rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Duress, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-threat-threshold`, `duress-target-scope`, `duress-imminence-proximity`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Edge Case Audit

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code. <!-- correct -->
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation rather than on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, and mere verbal threats of a beating cannot qualify as serious bodily injury.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats do not satisfy the "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person would have reported the threats to police before March 15, rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- edge-case-audit: CLEAN
1. Fact Pattern Booby Traps: pass (While the 5-day gap between the threat and the crime would ordinarily destroy the common-law duress defense based on lack of "imminence" and ample opportunity to escape, the prompt carefully circumvents this trap by explicitly instructing the student to "focus on the threat threshold and the target scope," and option (a) claims only that those specific elements are satisfied.)
2. Cross-Doctrine Clashes: pass (The felony murder charge arising from Bayes's death could preclude a common-law duress defense entirely depending on jurisdiction, but analyzing the elements in the context of the underlying robbery prevents this clash from breaking the question).
3. Cross-Question Spoilers: pass
Recommended fix: None required. The question uses careful constraining language to avoid the imminence and felony murder traps.
-->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code. <!-- correct -->
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation rather than on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, and mere verbal threats of a beating cannot qualify as serious bodily injury.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats do not satisfy the "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person would have reported the threats to police before March 15, rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option correctly maps the facts to the doctrinal elements. Under the common law, duress requires a threat of death or serious bodily injury (which a week-long hospital stay clearly satisfies) directed at the defendant or a close relation (which a mother satisfies). The Model Penal Code broadens these elements to "unlawful force" against "another," which is also plainly satisfied here. Thus, the threat threshold and target scope are met under both tests.
(b) Argument-for: A student might recall that common-law duress is highly restrictive compared to the Model Penal Code. By strictly construing the defense, a student could incorrectly deduce that traditional common law requires the threat to be directed solely at the defendant, making the threat against the mother legally immaterial for target scope purposes under the common law.
(c) Argument-for: Imminence is widely known as the hallmark of a common-law duress defense. Because Bentham's threat occurred five days before the robbery, a student could argue it constitutes a threat of future harm. Assuming that the Model Penal Code, while broader in target scope, maintains basic temporal proximity to prevent vigilantism or unnecessary crimes, a student could conclude that the MPC retains strict imminence as part of its threshold requirement.
(d) Argument-for: The common law sets an extremely high bar for duress. A student might interpret the common-law requirement of "death or grievous bodily harm" to practically mean an immediate, lethal threat—like a gun to the head. Under this strict (albeit exaggerated) view, Bentham's verbal threat of a future beating by a third party would fail the common-law threshold entirely, even while passing the MPC's looser "unlawful force" standard.
(e) Argument-for: Both the common law and the Model Penal Code require that the defendant's response meet an objective standard. With a five-day gap between the threat and the crime, an ordinary person of reasonable firmness would have sought police protection. A student could argue that this extended delay and failure to escape defeats the threat threshold as a matter of law, rendering the defense unavailable before it ever reaches a jury.

Head-to-head: Option (a) is correct because it accurately applies the elements of duress for both the common law and MPC to the facts. Every distractor employs an explicit false legal claim to justify its conclusion. Option (b) explicitly denies the "close relation" exception in common law. Option (c) explicitly claims the MPC retains the strict imminence requirement. Option (d) explicitly fabricates an "immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force" requirement for the common law. Option (e) conflates the failure to report/escape with the threshold element and falsely classifies the MPC's reasonable firmness test as failing "as a matter of law" in this scenario, rather than being a jury question.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law expressly recognizes threats against "close relations" (such as a mother).
- (c): "the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 explicitly drops the strict imminence requirement, replacing it with the broader "reasonable firmness" standard.
- (d): "the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force" — wrong because the common law also recognizes threats of serious or grievous bodily injury, not just deadly force.
- (e): "rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law" — wrong because under the common law, failure to report goes to the "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element rather than the threat threshold, and under the MPC, whether a person of reasonable firmness would have been coerced despite the delay is a factual question for the jury.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code. <!-- correct -->
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation rather than on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, and mere verbal threats of a beating cannot qualify as serious bodily injury.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats do not satisfy the "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person would have reported the threats to police before March 15, rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

## Q13

### Grounding

**Q13.** Assume that Kant, threatened by Bentham with serious bodily injury to Kant's mother, satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, during which his only role was driving the getaway van. Assume further that Bentham (not Kant) fired the fatal shot that killed Bayes. How does the defense of duress apply to Kant for felony murder under the common law, and for murder under the Model Penal Code?

(a) Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because the defense automatically applies whenever a defendant faces a threat of serious bodily injury to a relative.
(b) Duress is categorically unavailable for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because both legal frameworks enforce an absolute bar against asserting duress for any homicide offense.
(c) Under the common law *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception, duress is available for felony murder only if the defendant was unaware their co-felon was armed; under the Model Penal Code, duress is categorically barred for murder.
(d) Under the traditional common law, duress is barred for felony murder even for a non-killer participant, but Kant may successfully assert necessity because avoiding injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than Bayes's death.
(e) Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder, but the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for a non-killer participant who satisfies the defense for the predicate felony. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under the traditional common law, the duress defense is barred for all forms of murder, including felony murder. However, the modern *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for felony murder if the defendant is a non-killer participant whose involvement in the predicate felony was excused by duress. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 lacks a categorical homicide exception. (a) is incorrect because the traditional common law bars duress for murder. (b) is incorrect because the MPC allows duress as a defense to homicide. (c) is incorrect because the *Reichard* exception does not require ignorance of a co-felon's weapon, and the MPC does not bar duress for murder. (d) is incorrect because necessity (choice of evils) typically excludes homicide at common law, and avoiding physical injury is not legally recognized as a greater good than preserving a human life.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; People v. Reichard, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); McMillan v. State, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Felony-murder duress exception, Ch 21, Refinement `felony-murder-duress-exception`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q13.** Assume that Kant, threatened by Bentham with serious bodily injury to Kant's mother, satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, during which his only role was driving the getaway van. Assume further that Bentham (not Kant) fired the fatal shot that killed Bayes. How does the defense of duress apply to Kant for felony murder under the common law, and for murder under the Model Penal Code?

(a) Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because the defense automatically applies whenever a defendant faces a threat of serious bodily injury to a relative.
(b) Duress is categorically unavailable for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because both legal frameworks enforce an absolute bar against asserting duress for any homicide offense.
(c) Under the common law *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception, duress is available for felony murder only if the defendant was unaware their co-felon was armed; under the Model Penal Code, duress is categorically barred for murder.
(d) Under the traditional common law, duress is barred for felony murder even for a non-killer participant, but Kant may successfully assert necessity because avoiding injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than Bayes's death.
(e) Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder, but the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for a non-killer participant who satisfies the defense for the predicate felony. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under the traditional common law, the duress defense is barred for all forms of murder, including felony murder. However, the modern *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for felony murder if the defendant is a non-killer participant whose involvement in the predicate felony was excused by duress. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 lacks a categorical homicide exception. (a) is incorrect because the traditional common law bars duress for murder. (b) is incorrect because the MPC allows duress as a defense to homicide. (c) is incorrect because the *Reichard* exception does not require ignorance of a co-felon's weapon, and the MPC does not bar duress for murder. (d) is incorrect because necessity (choice of evils) typically excludes homicide at common law, and avoiding physical injury is not legally recognized as a greater good than preserving a human life.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; People v. Reichard, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); McMillan v. State, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under the MPC, duress is broadly available, and a student might mistakenly believe the modern common law matches this broad availability. The student could reason that because the threat was to a close relative (Kant's mother) and involved serious bodily injury, the elements of duress are so thoroughly satisfied that the defense overrides any homicide exclusions, making it "universally available."
(b) Argument-for: The traditional common law enforces a well-known, strict categorical bar on applying duress to any form of murder, requiring a person to sacrifice themselves rather than take an innocent life. A student could plausibly assume that the Model Penal Code, known for strict liability elements in some homicide areas, adopted this same historic, absolute moral bar against duress for murder.
(c) Argument-for: The *Reichard* and *McMillan* cases carved out exceptions for non-killer accomplices in felony murder. A student could logically infer that to avoid culpability for the homicide, the non-killer must lack foreseeability of the death, requiring them to be unaware the co-felon was armed. Furthermore, they might falsely assume the MPC categorically bars duress for murder as a standard choice-of-evils limitation.
(d) Argument-for: Under traditional common law, the murder bar strictly prevents duress from being applied to felony murder. A student might distinguish duress from necessity (choice of evils) and mistakenly argue that, under a utilitarian calculation, saving one's mother from "serious bodily injury" could be argued to a jury as a greater legal good than the life of the victim, especially if the victim's death was somewhat accidental or the relative's injury was certain.
(e) Argument-for: Traditional common law definitively bars duress for all murder, including felony murder. However, modern jurisprudence (*Reichard*/*McMillan*) established an exception allowing non-killer participants to use duress as a defense to felony murder if they were coerced into the underlying predicate felony. Moreover, Model Penal Code § 2.09 explicitly rejects the traditional common law murder bar, allowing duress for any crime, including murder, provided the defendant meets the "person of reasonable firmness" standard.

Head-to-head: 
Option (e) correctly integrates the traditional common law rule, the modern *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception, and the Model Penal Code's lack of a homicide bar. Option (a) uses absolute language ("universally available," "automatically applies") that wrongly ignores the traditional common law murder bar and the specific elements required to prove duress. Option (b) falsely claims the MPC enforces an absolute bar on duress for homicide, directly contradicting § 2.09. Option (c) relies on a fabricated requirement (unawareness of the weapon) for the *Reichard* exception and incorrectly asserts the MPC bars duress for murder. Option (d) incorrectly applies the necessity defense, as the common law never legally weighs avoiding serious bodily injury as a greater good than innocent human life. All distractors contain explicitly locked false legal claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law" and "automatically applies whenever" — wrong because traditional common law explicitly bars duress for murder, and the defense never "automatically" applies without meeting all specific elements (e.g., reasonable firmness).
- (b): "both legal frameworks enforce an absolute bar against asserting duress for any homicide offense" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 deliberately omits the common law homicide exception.
- (c): "under the Model Penal Code, duress is categorically barred for murder" — wrong because the MPC expressly permits duress for murder.
- (d): "avoiding injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than Bayes's death" — wrong because under the traditional common law choice of evils/necessity defense, preserving an innocent human life is always legally superior to (or at least equal to) avoiding physical injury.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q13.** Assume that Kant, threatened by Bentham with serious bodily injury to Kant's mother, satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, during which his only role was driving the getaway van. Assume further that Bentham (not Kant) fired the fatal shot that killed Bayes. How does the defense of duress apply to Kant for felony murder under the common law, and for murder under the Model Penal Code?

(a) Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because the defense automatically applies whenever a defendant faces a threat of serious bodily injury to a relative.
(b) Duress is categorically unavailable for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because both legal frameworks enforce an absolute bar against asserting duress for any homicide offense.
(c) Under the common law *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception, duress is available for felony murder only if the defendant was unaware their co-felon was armed; under the Model Penal Code, duress is categorically barred for murder.
(d) Under the traditional common law, duress is barred for felony murder even for a non-killer participant, but Kant may successfully assert necessity because avoiding injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than Bayes's death.
(e) Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder, but the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for a non-killer participant who satisfies the defense for the predicate felony. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under the traditional common law, the duress defense is barred for all forms of murder, including felony murder. However, the modern *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for felony murder if the defendant is a non-killer participant whose involvement in the predicate felony was excused by duress. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 lacks a categorical homicide exception. (a) is incorrect because the traditional common law bars duress for murder. (b) is incorrect because the MPC allows duress as a defense to homicide. (c) is incorrect because the *Reichard* exception does not require ignorance of a co-felon's weapon, and the MPC does not bar duress for murder. (d) is incorrect because necessity (choice of evils) typically excludes homicide at common law, and avoiding physical injury is not legally recognized as a greater good than preserving a human life.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; People v. Reichard, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); McMillan v. State, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under the MPC, duress is broadly available, and a student might mistakenly believe the modern common law matches this broad availability. The student could reason that because the threat was to a close relative (Kant's mother) and involved serious bodily injury, the elements of duress are so thoroughly satisfied that the defense overrides any homicide exclusions, making it "universally available."
(b) Argument-for: The traditional common law enforces a well-known, strict categorical bar on applying duress to any form of murder, requiring a person to sacrifice themselves rather than take an innocent life. A student could plausibly assume that the Model Penal Code, known for strict liability elements in some homicide areas, adopted this same historic, absolute moral bar against duress for murder.
(c) Argument-for: The *Reichard* and *McMillan* cases carved out exceptions for non-killer accomplices in felony murder. A student could logically infer that to avoid culpability for the homicide, the non-killer must lack foreseeability of the death, requiring them to be unaware the co-felon was armed. Furthermore, they might falsely assume the MPC categorically bars duress for murder as a standard choice-of-evils limitation.
(d) Argument-for: Under traditional common law, the murder bar strictly prevents duress from being applied to felony murder. A student might distinguish duress from necessity (choice of evils) and mistakenly argue that, under a utilitarian calculation, saving one's mother from "serious bodily injury" could be argued to a jury as a greater legal good than the life of the victim, especially if the victim's death was somewhat accidental or the relative's injury was certain.
(e) Argument-for: Traditional common law definitively bars duress for all murder, including felony murder. However, modern jurisprudence (*Reichard*/*McMillan*) established an exception allowing non-killer participants to use duress as a defense to felony murder if they were coerced into the underlying predicate felony. Moreover, Model Penal Code § 2.09 explicitly rejects the traditional common law murder bar, allowing duress for any crime, including murder, provided the defendant meets the "person of reasonable firmness" standard.

Head-to-head: 
Option (e) correctly integrates the traditional common law rule, the modern *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception, and the Model Penal Code's lack of a homicide bar. Option (a) uses absolute language ("universally available," "automatically applies") that wrongly ignores the traditional common law murder bar and the specific elements required to prove duress. Option (b) falsely claims the MPC enforces an absolute bar on duress for homicide, directly contradicting § 2.09. Option (c) relies on a fabricated requirement (unawareness of the weapon) for the *Reichard* exception and incorrectly asserts the MPC bars duress for murder. Option (d) incorrectly applies the necessity defense, as the common law never legally weighs avoiding serious bodily injury as a greater good than innocent human life. All distractors contain explicitly locked false legal claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law" and "automatically applies whenever" — wrong because traditional common law explicitly bars duress for murder, and the defense never "automatically" applies without meeting all specific elements (e.g., reasonable firmness).
- (b): "both legal frameworks enforce an absolute bar against asserting duress for any homicide offense" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 deliberately omits the common law homicide exception.
- (c): "under the Model Penal Code, duress is categorically barred for murder" — wrong because the MPC expressly permits duress for murder.
- (d): "avoiding injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than Bayes's death" — wrong because under the traditional common law choice of evils/necessity defense, preserving an innocent human life is always legally superior to (or at least equal to) avoiding physical injury.

Recommended fix: none

## Q14

### Grounding

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. Goetz*, evaluate whether Kant's belief that deadly force was immediately necessary was reasonable.

(a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted.

(b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry. <!-- correct -->

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly applies *Goetz*, which held that reasonableness incorporates the defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and specific knowledge of the attacker — not a purely external, context-stripped inquiry. The leveled revolver from six feet, the absence of any safe exit, and Bentham's prior explicit threat of hospitalizing violence all inform what a reasonable person in Kant's situation would have perceived. (a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Situational Incorporation / Objective Reasonableness, Ch 22, Refinement `situational-incorporation`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. Goetz*, evaluate whether Kant's belief that deadly force was immediately necessary was reasonable.

(a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted.

(b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry. <!-- correct -->

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly applies *Goetz*, which held that reasonableness incorporates the defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and specific knowledge of the attacker — not a purely external, context-stripped inquiry. The leveled revolver from six feet, the absence of any safe exit, and Bentham's prior explicit threat of hospitalizing violence all inform what a reasonable person in Kant's situation would have perceived. (a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that *Goetz* emphasized evaluating the circumstances from the defendant's perspective. The Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation captured the reality that the factfinder must step into the defendant's shoes. Because the Court of Appeals required the jury to consider the defendant's specific background, physical attributes, and prior experiences, a student might conclude that the highest court essentially adopted the "reasonable to him" standard in substance, even if it refined the nomenclature.
(b) Argument-for: A student could point out that *Goetz* famously established a hybrid objective-subjective standard for self-defense in New York. The Court held that while the belief must be objectively reasonable, the "reasonable person" must be placed in the defendant's exact "situation." This situation explicitly includes the physical realities of the encounter (a leveled gun, no cover) as well as the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker's prior threats. Thus, a reasonable person in Kant's exact situation could find deadly force immediately necessary.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue that *Goetz* explicitly rejected the Model Penal Code's subjective approach in favor of retaining an objective standard. To maintain a true objective standard, the inquiry must be cleansed of the defendant's subjective biases, fears, and idiosyncratic prior experiences. If the jury considers the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker, it risks converting the test back into the rejected subjective "reasonable to him" standard. Therefore, true objective reasonableness must exclude these subjective historical factors.
(d) Argument-for: A student might recall that the New York penal code revisions in the 1960s were heavily influenced by the Model Penal Code. The MPC deliberately abandoned the strict objective standard for self-defense, requiring only that the actor genuinely believe the force was necessary. A student could mistakenly believe that New York adopted this MPC provision wholesale, meaning Kant's honest, subjective belief is enough to satisfy the statute without an objective reasonableness requirement.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that when an actor is facing an immediate, terrifying threat like a leveled revolver, the situation triggers the "person of reasonable firmness" standard. This standard, widely used in necessity and duress contexts to evaluate objectively reasonable responses to overwhelming pressure, perfectly captures the terror of a gun pointed at one's chest. Thus, the controlling objective test is whether a reasonably firm person would succumb to the pressure and fire.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the clear correct answer, as it accurately applies the *Goetz* standard, which incorporates the defendant's "situation" (including prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker) into the objective reasonable person test. Option (a) fails because it explicitly states *Goetz* "adopted" the Appellate Division's standard, a historically and legally false claim since the Court of Appeals reversed the Appellate Division specifically to reject that formulation. Option (c) fails because it categorically asserts the standard "excludes any reference" to prior experiences, directly contradicting *Goetz*'s holding that the defendant's situation encompasses such experiences. Option (d) fails because it falsely claims New York adopted the "purely subjective formulation" of the MPC without requiring objective reasonableness, ignoring the legislature's addition of the word "reasonably." Option (e) fails because it incorrectly identifies the "person of reasonable firmness" test—which belongs to duress—as the controlling inquiry for self-defense. All distractors feature clear, falsifiable legal errors.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "*Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's 'reasonable to him' formulation" — wrong because the Court of Appeals expressly rejected the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" standard to preserve an objective requirement.
- (c): "excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker" — wrong because *Goetz* explicitly held that the objective standard must incorporate the physical circumstances and the defendant's prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker.
- (d): "New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation" — wrong because the New York legislature deliberately added the word "reasonably" to the MPC's language, requiring objective reasonableness.
- (e): "the controlling inquiry is whether a 'person of reasonable firmness' would have been unable to resist" — wrong because this formulation states the objective standard for the excuse of duress (MPC § 2.09), not the justification of self-defense.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q15

### Grounding

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity." Jurisdiction A requires retreat before using deadly force, but only if it can be accomplished "with complete personal safety." Jurisdiction B's Stand Your Ground statute imposes no duty to retreat unless the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity." Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. How would the duty-to-retreat analysis apply to Kant?

(a) In Jurisdiction B, Kant maintains his right to stand his ground because the "criminal activity" limitation applies only to crimes perpetrated against the initial aggressor.
(b) In Jurisdiction A, Kant must retreat because the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes a physically available escape route, which automatically satisfies the retreat standard.
(c) In Jurisdiction C, Kant must retreat because the jurisdiction's absolute no-retreat rule does not apply to commercial structures like a warehouse.
(d) In Jurisdiction A, Kant likely has no duty to retreat because a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver cannot be completed with complete safety; in Jurisdiction B, Kant loses the right to stand his ground because he is engaged in criminal activity; in Jurisdiction C, Kant has no duty to retreat. <!-- correct -->
(e) Kant is exempt from retreating in all three jurisdictions because the warehouse constitutes a workplace, which universally triggers the castle doctrine exception regardless of other statutory limits.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly applies each jurisdiction's provided rule. Jurisdiction A requires retreat only if available "with complete personal safety"; running fifteen feet across an open floor against a leveled revolver fails that standard. In Jurisdiction B, Kant loses the right to stand his ground because he is "engaged in criminal activity," as stipulated in the prompt. Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (a) fabricates a limitation to Jurisdiction B's categorical "criminal activity" bar. (b) ignores Jurisdiction A's "complete personal safety" requirement, treating mere physical availability as sufficient. (c) misapplies Jurisdiction C's rule, which explicitly applies in "any setting." (e) relies on a fabricated universal workplace castle-doctrine rule that overrides the specific jurisdictional rules provided.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (Duty to retreat limitations; "complete personal safety" standard; "engaged in criminal activity" SYG exclusions).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Duty to retreat and Stand Your Ground, Ch 22, Refinements `duty-to-retreat-general`, `stand-your-ground-statute`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity." Jurisdiction A requires retreat before using deadly force, but only if it can be accomplished "with complete personal safety." Jurisdiction B's Stand Your Ground statute imposes no duty to retreat unless the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity." Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. How would the duty-to-retreat analysis apply to Kant?

(a) In Jurisdiction B, Kant maintains his right to stand his ground because the "criminal activity" limitation applies only to crimes perpetrated against the initial aggressor.
(b) In Jurisdiction A, Kant must retreat because the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes a physically available escape route, which automatically satisfies the retreat standard.
(c) In Jurisdiction C, Kant must retreat because the jurisdiction's absolute no-retreat rule does not apply to commercial structures like a warehouse.
(d) In Jurisdiction A, Kant likely has no duty to retreat because a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver cannot be completed with complete safety; in Jurisdiction B, Kant loses the right to stand his ground because he is engaged in criminal activity; in Jurisdiction C, Kant has no duty to retreat. <!-- correct -->
(e) Kant is exempt from retreating in all three jurisdictions because the warehouse constitutes a workplace, which universally triggers the castle doctrine exception regardless of other statutory limits.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly applies each jurisdiction's provided rule. Jurisdiction A requires retreat only if available "with complete personal safety"; running fifteen feet across an open floor against a leveled revolver fails that standard.
